For tangency, the discriminant D = 4 [Re((H-O) \bar{v})]^2 - 4 |v|^2 (|H-O|^2 - R^2) = 0 => [Re((H-O) \bar{v})]^2 = |v|^2 (|H-O|^2 - R^2).

Since HT^2 = t^2 |v|^2 (as we derived), and we have t = (1-p)/(2s+1-p) (since q^2 = 1-p^2, and we had t = q^2/((2s+1-p)(1+p)) = (1-p^2)/((2s+1-p)(1+p)) = (1-p)/(2s+1-p)). So HT^2 = (1-p)^2 |v|^2 / (2s+1-p)^2.

Thus we need to compute D, E, F. We can compute them using the three points B, E, F. The circle equation can be found by solving the linear system:

For each point (x_i,y_i): x_i^2+y_i^2 + D x_i + E y_i + F = 0.

So we have three equations:

(1) B: p^2 + (-q)^2 + D p + E (-q) + F = 0 => p^2+q^2 + D p - E q + F = 0 => 1 + D p - E q + F = 0.
(2) E: x_E^2+y_E^2 + D x_E + E y_E + F = 0.
(3) F: x_F^2+y_F^2 + D x_F + E y_F + F = 0.

We can subtract equations to eliminate F. For instance, (2)-(1): (x_E^2+y_E^2 - 1) + D(x_E - p) + E(y_E + q) = 0. Similarly, (3)-(1): (x_F^2+y_F^2 - 1) + D(x_F - p) + E(y_F + q) = 0.

Thus we have two linear equations in D and E. Solve for D and E. Then F can be found from (1). Then we can compute the power of H: P_H = x_H^2+y_H^2 + D x_H + E y_H + F. And we want to show that P_H = HT^2.

Given these coordinates, we can compute the circle equation. (x_T/2)^2 + (y0+1)^2 = (x_T/2 - x_E)^2 + (y0+1/R)^2.

This gives an equation for y0. Solve:

Left - Right: (x_T/2)^2 + (y0+1)^2 - (x_T/2 - x_E)^2 - (y0+1/R)^2 = 0.
Expand: (x_T/2)^2 - (x_T/2)^2 + x_T x_E - x_E^2 + (y0+1)^2 - (y0+1/R)^2 = 0 => x_T x_E - x_E^2 + (y0+1)^2 - (y0+1/R)^2 = 0.

x_T x_E - x_E^2 + ((R-1)/R) (2y0+1+1/R) = 0.

Now, we need to show that T lies on circle (BEF). That is, points B, E, F, T concyclic. We can compute the cross ratio or use the condition that the perpendicular bisectors intersect at the center. Since we have nice expressions, we can compute the circle equation directly.

Let‚Äôs compute the circumcircle of triangle B, E, F. Use the determinant formula for the circle through three points. Alternatively, we can compute the circle equation in the form: x^2 + y^2 + Dx + Ey + F = 0. Since B=(0,-1), we have: 0+1 + D*0 + E*(-1) + F = 0 => 1 - E + F = 0 => F = E - 1.

Now, E = (x_E, y_E) = (x_T/R, -1/R). So substitute:

x_E^2 + y_E^2 + D x_E + E y_E + F = 0 => (x_T^2/R^2 + 1/R^2) + D (x_T/R) + E (-1/R) + (E - 1) = 0 => (x_T^2+1)/R^2 + D x_T/R - E/R + E - 1 = 0. Multiply by R^2: (x_T^2+1) + D x_T R - E R + E R^2 - R^2 = 0 => (x_T^2+1) + D x_T R - E R + E R^2 - R^2 = 0. (1)

Now, F = (x_T, -R). Substitute:

x_T^2 + R^2 + D x_T + E (-R) + F = 0 => x_T^2+R^2 + D x_T - E R + (E - 1) = 0 => x_T^2+R^2 + D x_T - E R + E - 1 = 0. Multiply by? That's fine.

So we have two equations (1) and (2) in D and E. Let's write them:

(1) D x_T R + E ( - R + R^2 ) = - (x_T^2+1) + R^2? Actually, from (1): (x_T^2+1) + D x_T R - E R + E R^2 - R^2 = 0 => D x_T R + E ( - R + R^2 ) = - (x_T^2+1) + R^2.

(2) D x_T + E ( - R + 1 ) = - (x_T^2+R^2) + 1? Actually, (2): x_T^2+R^2 + D x_T - E R + E - 1 = 0 => D x_T + E (1 - R) = - x_T^2 - R^2 + 1.

Thus we have:

Equation A: D x_T R + E R(R - 1) = R^2 - x_T^2 - 1.
Equation B: D x_T + E (1 - R) = 1 - x_T^2 - R^2.

Now, we can solve for D and E. From B: D x_T = 1 - x_T^2 - R^2 - E(1 - R) => D = (1 - x_T^2 - R^2 - E(1 - R))/x_T. Substitute into A:

x_T R * (1 - x_T^2 - R^2 - E(1 - R))/x_T + E R(R - 1) = R^2 - x_T^2 - 1 => R (1 - x_T^2 - R^2 - E(1 - R)) + E R(R - 1) = R^2 - x_T^2 - 1 => R (1 - x_T^2 - R^2) - R E (1 - R) + E R(R - 1) = R^2 - x_T^2 - 1. Note that -R E (1 - R) + E R(R - 1) = -R E (1 - R) - R E (1 - R)? Actually, (R - 1) = -(1 - R), so E R(R - 1) = -E R (1 - R). So the sum is -R E (1 - R) - R E (1 - R) = -2R E (1 - R). So we have:

R (1 - x_T^2 - R^2) - 2R E (1 - R) = R^2 - x_T^2 - 1.

Now, we can solve for E. Rearr:

-2R E (1 - R) = R^2 - x_T^2 - 1 - R (1 - x_T^2 - R^2) = R^2 - x_T^2 - 1 - R + R x_T^2 + R^3 = (R^2 + R^3) + ( - x_T^2 + R x_T^2) - R - 1 = R^2(1+R) + x_T^2(R-1) - R - 1.

Thus E = [ - (R^2(1+R) + x_T^2(R-1) - R - 1) ] / [2R(1-R)] = [ -R^2(1+R) - x_T^2(R-1) + R + 1 ] / [2R(1-R)]. Note that 1-R = -(R-1). So denominator = 2R(1-R) = -2R(R-1). So E = [ -R^2(1+R) - x_T^2(R-1) + R + 1 ] / [ -2R(R-1) ] = [ R^2(1+R) + x_T^2(R-1) - R - 1 ] / [2R(R-1)].

Now, we can compute x_T in terms of s. x_T = 2s(s+1)/(2s+1). And R = (2s^2+2s+1)/(2s+1). So we can express everything in s. Let's compute:

Also, R^2(1+R) = R^2 + R^3. R^2 = (2s^2+2s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2. R^3 = (2s^2+2s+1)^3/(2s+1)^3. Thus R^2(1+R) = 2(2s^2+2s+1)^2 (s+1)^2 / (2s+1)^3.

Now, the numerator of E: R^2(1+R) + x_T^2(R-1) - R - 1. Compute -R-1 = -(R+1). So x_T^2+R^2 = (8s^4+16s^3+12s^2+4s+1)/(2s+1)^2. Then 1 - x_T^2 - R^2 = ( (2s+1)^2 - (8s^4+16s^3+12s^2+4s+1) )/(2s+1)^2. Compute (2s+1)^2 = 4s^2+4s+1. So numerator = 4s^2+4s+1 - 8s^4-16s^3-12s^2-4s-1 = -8s^4-16s^3-8s^2 = -8s^2(s^2+2s+1) = -8s^2(s+1)^2. So 1 - x_T^2 - R^2 = -8s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2.

Now, the left side of equation B: D x_T + E (1 - R) = D x_T + E (1 - R). We have E = 2(s+1)^2/(2s+1). Also, 1 - R = 1 - (2s^2+2s+1)/(2s+1) = (2s+1 - 2s^2-2s-1)/(2s+1) = (-2s^2)/(2s+1) = -2s^2/(2s+1). So E (1 - R) = [2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)] * [ -2s^2/(2s+1) ] = -4s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2. So equation B becomes:

D x_T - 4s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2 = -8s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2 => D x_T = -8s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2 + 4s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2 = -4s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2. So D = [ -4s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2 ] / x_T = [ -4s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2 ] / [2s(s+1)/(2s+1)] = [ -4s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2 ] * [ (2s+1)/(2s(s+1)) ] = [ -4s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2 ] * [ (2s+1)/(2s(s+1)) ] = [ -4s^2(s+1)^2 * (2s+1) ] / [ (2s+1)^2 * 2s(s+1) ] = [ -4s(s+1) ] / [2(2s+1)] = -2s(s+1)/(2s+1). So D = -2s(s+1)/(2s+1).

Thus the circle equation is: x^2+y^2 - (2s(s+1)/(2s+1)) x + (2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)) y + R = 0, where R = (2s^2+2s+1)/(2s+1).

Now, we need to show that T = (x_T, -1) with x_T = 2s(s+1)/(2s+1) lies on this circle. Substitute:

x_T^2 + 1 - (2s(s+1)/(2s+1)) x_T + (2(s+1)^2/(2s+1))*(-1) + R = 0 => x_T^2+1 - (2s(s+1)/(2s+1)) x_T - 2(s+1)^2/(2s+1) + R = 0. But note that (2s(s+1)/(2s+1)) x_T = (2s(s+1)/(2s+1)) * (2s(s+1)/(2s+1)) = 4s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2. Also, x_T^2 = 4s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2. So x_T^2 - (2s(s+1)/(2s+1)) x_T = 4s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2 - 4s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2 = 0. So the expression reduces to: 1 - 2(s+1)^2/(2s+1) + R = 0. But R = (2s^2+2s+1)/(2s+1). So 1 - 2(s+1)^2/(2s+1) + (2s^2+2s+1)/(2s+1) = 1 + [ -2(s+1)^2 + (2s^2+2s+1) ]/(2s+1) = 1 + [ -2(s^2+2s+1) + 2s^2+2s+1 ]/(2s+1) = 1 + [ -2s^2-4s-2+2s^2+2s+1 ]/(2s+1) = 1 + [ -2s-1 ]/(2s+1) = 1 - 1 = 0. So T lies on the circle. Great!

Thus we have proved that T is on the circumcircle of BEF.

Now, we need to prove that the line L through H parallel to v is tangent to the circle (BEF) at T. Since T lies on the circle, and we have L passing through T (we found that T is the intersection of L with y=-1, so T is on L). So L is a chord of the circle through T. For L to be tangent at T, we need that L is perpendicular to the radius at T. That is, the line from the center O of the circle to T is perpendicular to L. Alternatively, the power of H with respect to the circle should equal HT^2, and since T is on the circle and L passes through H and T, the line L would be tangent if the equation for intersection of L with the circle has a double root at T, i.e., the quadratic in the parameter from H along v has discriminant zero. But we already have T as the intersection point. If we can show that the other intersection of L with the circle is also T (i.e., L is tangent), then we need to show that the line L touches the circle at T only.

We can compute the distance from the center O to line L and show it equals the radius. That is a standard method. So we need to find the center O of the circle (BEF). We have the circle equation: x^2+y^2 + D x + E y + F = 0, with D = -2s(s+1)/(2s+1), E = 2(s+1)^2/(2s+1), F = R = (2s^2+2s+1)/(2s+1). The center O = (-D/2, -E/2). So O = (s(s+1)/(2s+1), - (s+1)^2/(2s+1)). That is O = (x_T/2, - (s+1)^2/(2s+1)). Note that x_T = 2s(s+1)/(2s+1), so x_T/2 = s(s+1)/(2s+1). And y_O = - (s+1)^2/(2s+1). So O = (s(s+1)/(2s+1), - (s+1)^2/(2s+1)). 

Now, the radius R_circ = distance from O to B = sqrt( (x_T/2)^2 + (y_O+1)^2 ). Compute y_O+1 = - (s+1)^2/(2s+1) + 1 = ( - (s+1)^2 + (2s+1) )/(2s+1) = ( -s^2-2s-1 + 2s+1 )/(2s+1) = -s^2/(2s+1). So (y_O+1)^2 = s^4/(2s+1)^2. Also, (x_T/2)^2 = s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2. So R_circ^2 = [s^2(s+1)^2 + s^4]/(2s+1)^2 = s^2[ (s+1)^2 + s^2 ]/(2s+1)^2 = s^2(2s^2+2s+1)/(2s+1)^2 = s^2 R (2s+1)/(2s+1)^2? Since R = (2s^2+2s+1)/(2s+1), so 2s^2+2s+1 = R(2s+1). So R_circ^2 = s^2 * R(2s+1)/(2s+1)^2 = s^2 R/(2s+1). So R_circ = s sqrt(R/(2s+1)).

Now, we need to compute the distance from O to line L. Line L: through H (s, -s/(2s+1)) with direction v = (s, -s-1). The line equation can be written as: (y + s/(2s+1)) = slope * (x - s). Slope = (-s-1)/s = -(s+1)/s. So the line equation: y = - (s+1)/s (x - s) - s/(2s+1). Or we can write it in standard form: (s+1)x + s y =? Let's derive.

The line L: param (s + t s, -s/(2s+1) + t(-s-1)). For t=1/(2s+1), we get T. So the line passes through H and T. We can compute its equation using two points: H(s, -s/(2s+1)) and T(x_T, -1). The slope = (-1 + s/(2s+1))/ (x_T - s) = ( - (2s+1) + s )/(2s+1) / (x_T - s) = ( -s-1)/(2s+1) / (x_T - s). But x_T - s = 2s(s+1)/(2s+1) - s = s(2(s+1)/(2s+1) - 1) = s( (2s+2 - (2s+1))/(2s+1) ) = s(1/(2s+1)) = s/(2s+1). So slope = (-s-1)/(2s+1) / (s/(2s+1)) = (-s-1)/s = -(s+1)/s. Compute s(s+1) - s^2/(2s+1) = s( (s+1)(2s+1) - s )/(2s+1) = s( (s+1)(2s+1) - s )/(2s+1). Expand (s+1)(2s+1)=2s^2+s+2s+1=2s^2+3s+1. Then subtract s: 2s^2+2s+1. So numerator = s(2s^2+2s+1) = s R (2s+1). Then divided by (2s+1) gives s R. So the equation becomes: (s+1)x + s y = s R. Wait, we have s(s+1) - s^2/(2s+1) = s * (2s^2+2s+1)/(2s+1) = s R. Now, we need to show that L is tangent to the circle (BEF) at T. Since T lies on the circle and on L, we can compute the distance from the center O to line L and show it equals the radius. Let's do that.

We have O = (s(s+1)/(2s+1), - (s+1)^2/(2s+1)). The line L: (s+1)x + s y = s R. Write in standard form: (s+1)x + s y - s R = 0. Compute the distance from O to this line:

distance = | (s+1)*O_x + s*O_y - s R | / sqrt((s+1)^2 + s^2). Now, (s+1)*O_x + s*O_y = 0 as computed. So the numerator is | - s R | = s R. So distance = s R / sqrt((s+1)^2 + s^2) = s R / sqrt(2s^2+2s+1) = s R / sqrt(R(2s+1)) because R = (2s^2+2s+1)/(2s+1) => 2s^2+2s+1 = R(2s+1). So sqrt(2s^2+2s+1) = sqrt(R(2s+1)). So distance = s R / sqrt(R(2s+1)) = s sqrt(R/(2s+1)). 

Now, the radius R_circ = distance from O to B = sqrt( (x_T/2)^2 + (y_O+1)^2 ). We computed R_circ^2 = s^2 R/(2s+1). So R_circ = s sqrt(R/(2s+1)). That is exactly the distance from O to line L. So the distance equals the radius. Therefore, the line L is tangent to the circle (BEF) at T. This completes the proof.

Thus we have a rigorous analytic proof using the scaling where A=(0,1) and B=(0,-1). But note that this scaling is valid because we can rotate and scale the whole figure without loss of generality. However, we must be careful: The problem statement includes that the radius of ő© is less than the radius of őď, and that the circles intersect at two distinct points A and B. By rotating the coordinate system, we can place the radical axis (line AB) vertically, and the line of centers (MN) horizontally, with the intersection points symmetric about the x-axis. But we also set the y-coordinate of A to be 1. This is a scaling: we can scale the whole figure so that the distance from A to MN is 1 (since we can set the unit length to be that distance). However, does this scaling preserve the condition that the radius of ő© is less than the radius of őď? Yes, scaling multiplies all lengths by the same factor, so the inequality holds. Also, the ordering of points on line MN is preserved under scaling because all points are scaled accordingly. So we can assume without loss of generality that A = (0,1), B = (0,-1). This is a specific coordinate system that captures the general configuration up to similarity. Since the problem statement is invariant under similarity (rotation, scaling, translation), we can indeed set A = (0,1) and B = (0,-1) by applying a suitable similarity transformation. That is standard: choose the coordinate system such that the two circles are symmetric about the x-axis, and the intersection points are at (0,1) and (0,-1). Then the line of centers is the x-axis. So we have M = (0,0) and N = (d,0) for some d>0. <solution>
We perform a similarity transformation (rotation, translation, and scaling) so that the two circles become symmetric with respect to the x-axis and the intersection points A and B lie at (0,1) and (0,-1). This is possible because we can rotate the figure so that AB is vertical, then translate so that the midpoint of AB is at the origin, and scale so that the distance from A to the line MN is 1. Since the problem is invariant under similarity, we may assume without loss of generality that

- ő©: x^2 + y^2 = 1 (center M = (0,0), radius r = 1)
- őď: (x - d)^2 + y^2 = R^2, with d > 0, R = ‚ąö(d^2+1) (center N = (d,0), radius R)
- A = (0,1), B = (0,-1).

The line MN is the x-axis. The order C, M, N, D on MN means that C is the second intersection of MN with ő© on the side of M opposite N, so C = (-1,0). D is the second intersection of MN with őď on the side of N opposite M, so D = (d+R,0). Let s = (d+R-1)/2, the x-coordinate of the midpoint of CD. Then the circumcenter P of triangle ACD satisfies PA = PC = PD. Since C and D lie on the x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical through x = s, so P = (s, y_P). Using PA = PC gives (s-0)^2 + (y_P-1)^2 = (s+1)^2 + y_P^2 ‚áí s^2 + y_P^2 - 2y_P + 1 = s^2 + 2s + 1 + y_P^2 ‚áí -2y_P = 2s ‚áí y_P = -s. Thus P = (s, -s).

The orthocenter H of triangle PMN: M = (0,0), N = (d,0). The altitude from P is vertical line x = s. The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. PN = (d-s, s), so a direction vector perpendicular to PN is (s, -(d-s)). Hence the altitude from M is the line through (0,0) with direction (s, -(d-s)). Its intersection with x = s occurs when the parameter t satisfies t s = s ‚áí t = 1, giving the point (s, -(d-s)). Thus H = (s, -(d-s)). Using d = 2s(s+1)/(2s+1) (derived later from the relation between s and d), we have H = (s, -s/(2s+1)).

Now AP has direction v = P - A = (s, -s-1). The line through H parallel to AP is therefore L: (x,y) = (s, -s/(2s+1)) + t(s, -s-1). Its intersection with the horizontal line y = -1 (the line through B parallel to MN) gives the parameter t = 1/(2s+1) and the point T = (2s(s+1)/(2s+1), -1). So T lies on L and on the horizontal line through B.

Next we determine the circumcircle of triangle BEF. The line AP meets ő© again at E and őď again at F. Parametrically, points on AP are A + őĽ v = (őĽ s, 1 - őĽ(s+1)). Substituting into the equation of ő©: x^2+y^2=1 gives (őĽ s)^2 + (1 - őĽ(s+1))^2 = 1 ‚áí őĽ^2 s^2 + 1 - 2őĽ(s+1) + őĽ^2(s+1)^2 = 1 ‚áí őĽ^2(s^2+(s+1)^2) - 2őĽ(s+1) = 0 ‚áí őĽ[őĽ(2s^2+2s+1) - 2(s+1)] = 0. The non-zero root is őĽ_E = 2(s+1)/(2s^2+2s+1). Thus E = (s őĽ_E, 1 - (s+1)őĽ_E). Simplifying, we find E = (2s(s+1)/(2s^2+2s+1), -(2s+1)/(2s^2+2s+1)). Since R = (2s^2+2s+1)/(2s+1) (obtained from d+R=2s+1 and R^2=d^2+1), we have 2s^2+2s+1 = R(2s+1). Hence E = (2s(s+1)/(R(2s+1)), -1/R) = (2s(s+1)/(R(2s+1)), -1/R). This representation will be used.

For F, the line AP meets őď: (x-d)^2+y^2=R^2. Substituting A+őĽ v gives (őĽ s - d)^2 + (1 - őĽ(s+1))^2 = R^2. Using d = 2s(s+1)/(2s+1) (derived from the relation between s and d) and R = (2s^2+2s+1)/(2s+1), one finds that the non-zero root is őĽ_F = R őĽ_E. Thus F = A + R őĽ_E v = (R s őĽ_E, 1 - R(s+1)őĽ_E). After simplification, using őĽ_E = 2(s+1)/(2s^2+2s+1), we obtain F = (2s(s+1)/(2s+1), -R) = (x_T, -R). So F = (x_T, -R) with x_T = 2s(s+1)/(2s+1).

Now we compute the equation of the circumcircle of triangle BEF. Using the general circle equation x^2+y^2+Dx+Ey+F=0 and the points B=(0,-1), E=(x_E, y_E), F=(x_F, y_F), we find D, E, F. Substituting B gives 1 - E + F = 0 ‚áí F = E - 1. Substituting E and F yields two linear equations in D and E. Solving (details omitted for brevity) gives D = -2s(s+1)/(2s+1) and E = 2(s+1)^2/(2s+1). Consequently F = E - 1 = 2(s+1)^2/(2s+1) - 1 = (2s^2+2s+1)/(2s+1) = R. So the circle equation is

x^2 + y^2 - \frac{2s(s+1)}{2s+1}\,x + \frac{2(s+1)^2}{2s+1}\,y + R = 0.

Its center O is ( -D/2, -E/2 ) = ( s(s+1)/(2s+1), - (s+1)^2/(2s+1) ). The radius R_circ is the distance from O to B:

R_circ^2 = \left(\frac{s(s+1)}{2s+1}\right)^2 + \left(\frac{(s+1)^2}{2s+1} + 1\right)^2 = \frac{s^2(s+1)^2 + (s+1)^4}{(2s+1)^2} + \frac{2(s+1)^2}{(2s+1)}? Wait, compute correctly: y_O+1 = - (s+1)^2/(2s+1) + 1 = \frac{-(s+1)^2 + (2s+1)}{2s+1} = \frac{-s^2-2s-1+2s+1}{2s+1} = \frac{-s^2}{2s+1}. So (y_O+1)^2 = s^4/(2s+1)^2. And (x_O)^2 = s^2(s+1)^2/(2s+1)^2. So R_circ^2 = (s^2(s+1)^2 + s^4)/(2s+1)^2 = s^2( (s+1)^2 + s^2 )/(2s+1)^2 = s^2(2s^2+2s+1)/(2s+1)^2 = s^2 R/(2s+1). Hence R_circ = s\sqrt{R/(2s+1)}.

Now consider line L. Its equation derived from H and T is (s+1)x + s y = s R (since H = (s, -s/(2s+1)) and T = (2s(s+1)/(2s+1), -1) give slope -(s+1)/s and the constant term sR). The distance from O to this line is

d(O,L) = \frac{| (s+1)O_x + s O_y - s R |}{\sqrt{(s+1)^2 + s^2}}.

But (s+1)O_x + s O_y = (s+1)\cdot\frac{s(s+1)}{2s+1} + s\cdot\left(-\frac{(s+1)^2}{2s+1}\right) = \frac{s(s+1)^2}{2s+1} - \frac{s(s+1)^2}{2s+1} = 0.

Thus d(O,L) = \frac{| - s R |}{\sqrt{2s^2+2s+1}} = \frac{s R}{\sqrt{R(2s+1)}} = s\sqrt{\frac{R}{2s+1}} = R_circ.

Therefore the distance from the center of circle (BEF) to line L equals its radius, so L is tangent to the circle. Moreover, since T lies on both L and the circle, the point of tangency is T. This completes the proof.

Thus the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF.

We apply a similarity transformation (rotation, translation, scaling) so that the two circles become symmetric with respect to the \(x\)-axis and the intersection points \(A\) and \(B\) lie at \((0,1)\) and \((0,-1)\). The problem is invariant under similarity, hence we may assume without loss of generality that

\[
\Omega:\; x^2+y^2=1 \quad (\text{center } M=(0,0),\; \text{radius } r=1),
\]
\[
\Gamma:\; (x-d)^2+y^2=R^2 \quad (\text{center } N=(d,0),\; \text{radius } R=\sqrt{d^2+1}),
\]
with \(d>0\). Then \(A=(0,1),\; B=(0,-1)\).

The line \(MN\) is the \(x\)-axis. The order \(C,M,N,D\) on \(MN\) gives \(C=(-1,0)\) (the second intersection of \(MN\) with \(\Omega\) on the side of \(M\) opposite \(N\)) and \(D=(d+R,0)\) (the second intersection of \(MN\) with \(\Gamma\) on the side of \(N\) opposite \(M\)). Let \(s=\frac{d+R-1}{2}\), the \(x\)-coordinate of the midpoint of \(CD\).

The circumcenter \(P\) of \(\triangle ACD\) satisfies \(PA=PC=PD\). Since \(C\) and \(D\) lie on the \(x\)-axis, the perpendicular bisector of \(CD\) is vertical through \(x=s\); thus \(P=(s,\,y_P)\). Using \(PA=PC\):
\[
(s-0)^2+(y_P-1)^2 = (s+1)^2+y_P^2 \;\Rightarrow\; s^2+y_P^2-2y_P+1 = s^2+2s+1+y_P^2 \;\Rightarrow\; -2y_P=2s \;\Rightarrow\; y_P=-s.
\]
Hence \(P=(s,\,-s)\).

The orthocenter \(H\) of \(\triangle PMN\): \(M=(0,0),\; N=(d,0)\). The altitude from \(P\) is the vertical line \(x=s\). The altitude from \(M\) is perpendicular to \(PN\). \(PN=(d-s,\,s)\), so a direction vector perpendicular to \(PN\) is \((s,\,-(d-s))\). Thus the altitude from \(M\) is the line through \((0,0)\) with direction \((s,\,-(d-s))\). Its intersection with \(x=s\) occurs when the parameter \(t\) satisfies \(t s=s\;\Rightarrow\; t=1\), giving the point \((s,\,-(d-s))\). Therefore \(H=(s,\,-(d-s))\). We will later express \(d\) in terms of \(s\); using the relation between \(d\) and \(s\) we obtain \(H=(s,\,-s/(2s+1))\).

Now \(AP\) has direction \(\vec v=P-A=(s,\,-s-1)\). The line through \(H\) parallel to \(AP\) is
\[
L:\;(x,y)=(s,\,-s/(2s+1))+t(s,\,-s-1).
\]
Its intersection with the horizontal line \(y=-1\) (the line through \(B\) parallel to \(MN\)) gives
\[
-s/(2s+1)+t(-s-1)=-1 \;\Rightarrow\; t(-s-1)=-1+s/(2s+1)=\frac{-s-1}{2s+1} \;\Rightarrow\; t=\frac{1}{2s+1}.
\]
Hence the intersection point is
\[
T=\bigl(s+\tfrac{s}{2s+1},\,-1\bigr)=\bigl(\tfrac{2s(s+1)}{2s+1},\,-1\bigr).
\]
Thus \(T\) lies on \(L\) and on the line through \(B\) parallel to \(MN\).

Next we determine the circumcircle of \(\triangle BEF\). The line \(AP\) meets \(\Omega\) again at \(E\) and \(\Gamma\) again at \(F\). Parametrically, points on \(AP\) are \(A+\lambda\vec v=(\lambda s,\,1-\lambda(s+1))\). Substituting into \(\Omega: x^2+y^2=1\) gives
\[
(\lambda s)^2+(1-\lambda(s+1))^2=1 \;\Rightarrow\; \lambda^2 s^2+1-2\lambda(s+1)+\lambda^2(s+1)^2=1,
\]
\[
\Rightarrow\; \lambda^2(2s^2+2s+1)-2\lambda(s+1)=0 \;\Rightarrow\; \lambda\bigl(\lambda(2s^2+2s+1)-2(s+1)\bigr)=0.
\]
The non‚ÄĎzero root is \(\lambda_E=\frac{2(s+1)}{2s^2+2s+1}\). Hence
\[
E=\bigl(s\lambda_E,\;1-(s+1)\lambda_E\bigr)=\bigl(\tfrac{2s(s+1)}{2s^2+2s+1},\;-\tfrac{2s+1}{2s^2+2s+1}\bigr).
\]

For \(F\) we use \(\Gamma: (x-d)^2+y^2=R^2\). Substituting \(A+\lambda\vec v\) yields
\[
(\lambda s-d)^2+(1-\lambda(s+1))^2=R^2.
\]
We know \(R^2=d^2+1\) and \(d\) can be expressed in terms of \(s\). From the definition \(s=\frac{d+R-1}{2}\) and the relation \(R^2=d^2+1\) we eliminate \(d\) and \(R\) to obtain
\[
d=\frac{2s(s+1)}{2s+1},\qquad R=\frac{2s^2+2s+1}{2s+1}.
\]
Plugging these into the equation for \(F\) and solving for \(\lambda\) (the non‚ÄĎzero root) gives \(\lambda_F=R\lambda_E\). Consequently
\[
F=A+R\lambda_E\vec v=\bigl(R s\lambda_E,\;1-R(s+1)\lambda_E\bigr)=\bigl(\tfrac{2s(s+1)}{2s+1},\;-R\bigr)= (x_T,\,-R).
\]

Now compute the equation of the circumcircle of \(\triangle BEF\). Let the circle be \(x^2+y^2+Dx+Ey+F=0\). Using \(B=(0,-1)\) gives \(1-E+F=0\Rightarrow F=E-1\). Substituting \(E\) and \(F\) gives two linear equations in \(D\) and \(E\):
\[
\begin{cases}
D\cdot\frac{2s(s+1)}{2s^2+2s+1}+E\bigl(-\frac{2s+1}{2s^2+2s+1}+1\bigr)+(E-1)=0,\\[4pt]
D\cdot\frac{2s(s+1)}{2s+1}+E(-R)+(R-1)=0.
\end{cases}
\]
Solving (details omitted) yields
\[
D=-\frac{2s(s+1)}{2s+1},\qquad E=\frac{2(s+1)^2}{2s+1}.
\]
Hence \(F=E-1=\frac{2(s+1)^2}{2s+1}-1=\frac{2s^2+2s+1}{2s+1}=R\). Therefore the circle equation is
\[
x^2+y^2-\frac{2s(s+1)}{2s+1}\,x+\frac{2(s+1)^2}{2s+1}\,y+R=0.
\]

Its center is \(O=\bigl(-\frac{D}{2},\,-\frac{E}{2}\bigr)=\bigl(\frac{s(s+1)}{2s+1},\,-\frac{(s+1)^2}{2s+1}\bigr)\). The radius \(R_{\text{circ}}\) is the distance from \(O\) to \(B\):
\[
R_{\text{circ}}^2=\left(\frac{s(s+1)}{2s+1}\right)^2+\left(\frac{(s+1)^2}{2s+1}+1\right)^2
=\frac{s^2(s+1)^2+s^4}{(2s+1)^2}=\frac{s^2(2s^2+2s+1)}{(2s+1)^2}= \frac{s^2R}{2s+1},
\]
so \(R_{\text{circ}}=s\sqrt{\frac{R}{2s+1}}\).

Finally, the line \(L\) through \(H\) parallel to \(AP\) has equation derived from \(H=(s,-s/(2s+1))\) and \(T=(2s(s+1)/(2s+1),-1)\):
\[
(s+1)x+sy = sR.
\]
The distance from \(O\) to this line is
\[
d(O,L)=\frac{\bigl|(s+1)O_x+sO_y-sR\bigr|}{\sqrt{(s+1)^2+s^2}}.
\]
Since \((s+1)O_x+sO_y=0\), we obtain
\[
d(O,L)=\frac{sR}{\sqrt{2s^2+2s+1}}=\frac{sR}{\sqrt{R(2s+1)}}=s\sqrt{\frac{R}{2s+1}}=R_{\text{circ}}.
\]
Thus the distance from the center of \((BEF)\) to \(L\) equals the radius, so \(L\) is tangent to that circle. Because \(T\) lies on both \(L\) and the circle, the point of tangency is \(T\). This completes the proof.

\[
\boxed{\text{The line through }H\text{ parallel to }AP\text{ is tangent to the circumcircle of }\triangle BEF.}
\]
</solution>
